class="plainlist">/n<ul>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Tony_Hoare" title="Tony Hoare">Tony Hoare</a><sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup></li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Niklaus_Wirth" title="Niklaus Wirth">Niklaus Wirth</a><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup></li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Leslie_Lamport" title="Leslie Lamport">Leslie Lamport</a><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lamport_.282015.29_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lamport_.282015.29-7">[7]</a></sup></li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/David_Gries" title="David Gries">David Gries</a></li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/David_Parnas" title="David Parnas">David Parnas</a></li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Shlomi_Dolev" title="Shlomi Dolev">Shlomi Dolev</a></li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Alexander_Stepanov" title="Alexander Stepanov">Alexander Stepanov</a><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></li>/n</ul>/n</div>/n</td>/n</tr>/n<tr style="display:none">/n<td colspan="2"></td>/n</tr>/n</table>/n<p><b>Edsger Wybe Dijkstra</b> (<small>Dutch:&#160;</small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Dutch" title="Help:IPA/Dutch">[ˈɛtsxər ˈʋibə ˈdɛikstra]</a></span><small class="nowrap">&#160;(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/File:Dijkstra.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20" /></a>&#160;</span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/85/Dijkstra.ogg" class="internal" title="Dijkstra.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</small>; 11 May 1930 – 6 August 2002) was a Dutch <a href="/wiki/Computer_scientist" title="Computer scientist">computer scientist</a><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> and an early <a href="/wiki/List_of_pioneers_in_computer_science" title="List of pioneers in computer science">pioneer</a> in many research areas of <a href="/wiki/Computing_science" class="mw-redirect" title="Computing science">computing science</a> who held the Schlumberger Centennial Chair in Computer Sciences at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Texas_at_Austin" title="University of Texas at Austin">University of Texas at Austin</a> from 1984 until his retirement in 1999. A theoretical physicist by training, he worked as a <a href="/wiki/Programmer" title="Programmer">programmer</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Mathematisch_Centrum" class="mw-redirect" title="Mathematisch Centrum">Mathematisch Centrum</a> (Amsterdam) from 1952 to 1962. He was a professor of mathematics at the <a href="/wiki/Eindhoven_University_of_Technology" title="Eindhoven University of Technology">Eindhoven University of Technology</a> (1962–1984) and a research fellow at the <a href="/wiki/Burroughs_Corporation" title="Burroughs Corporation">Burroughs Corporation</a> (1973–1984).</p>/n<p>One of the most influential members of computing science's <a href="/wiki/List_of_pioneers_in_computer_science" title="List of pioneers in computer science">founding generation</a>, Dijkstra helped shape the new discipline from both an engineering and a <a href="/wiki/Theoretical_computer_science" title="Theoretical computer science">theoretical perspective</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Dijkstra_bio_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dijkstra_bio-10">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Many of his papers are the source of new research areas. Several concepts and problems that are now standard in computer science were first identified by Dijkstra or bear names coined by him.<sup id="cite_ref-Apt.2C_Krzysztof_R._2002_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Apt.2C_Krzysztof_R._2002-12">[12]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>/n<p><a href="/wiki/Computer_programming" title="Computer programming">Computer programming</a> in the 1950s to 1960s was not recognized as an academic discipline; Dijkstra, who had a background in mathematics and physics, was one of the driving forces behind the acceptance of computer programming as a <a href="/wiki/Scientific_discipline" class="mw-redirect" title="Scientific discipline">scientific discipline</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-DijkstraNYT_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DijkstraNYT-14">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Dijkstra coined the phrase "<a href="/wiki/Structured_programming" title="Structured programming">structured programming</a>" and during the 1970s this became the new programming orthodoxy.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Dijkstra's ideas about structured programming helped lay the foundations for the birth and development of the professional discipline of <a href="/wiki/Software_engineering" title="Software engineering">software engineering</a>, enabling programmers to organize and manage increasingly complex software projects.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>/n<p>The academic study of <a href="/wiki/Concurrent_computing" title="Concurrent computing">concurrent computing</a> started in the 1960s, with Dijkstra (1965) credited with being the first paper in this field, identifying and solving the <a href="/wiki/Mutual_exclusion" title="Mutual exclusion">mutual exclusion</a> problem.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> He was also one of the early pioneers of the research on principles of <a href="/wiki/Distributed_computing" title="Distributed computing">distributed computing</a>. His foundational work on <a href="/wiki/Concurrency_(computer_science)" title="Concurrency (computer science)">concurrency</a>, <a href="/wiki/Semaphore_(programming)" title="Semaphore (programming)">semaphores</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mutual_exclusion" title="Mutual exclusion">mutual exclusion</a> (<a href="/wiki/Mutex" class="mw-redirect" title="Mutex">mutex</a>), <a href="/wiki/Deadlock" title="Deadlock">deadlock</a> (<a href="/wiki/Deadly_embrace" class="mw-redirect" title="Deadly embrace">deadly embrace</a>), finding <a href="/wiki/Shortest_path_problem" title="Shortest path problem">shortest paths in graphs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fault-tolerance" class="mw-redirect" title="Fault-tolerance">fault-tolerance</a>, <a href="/wiki/Self-stabilization" title="Self-stabilization">self-stabilization</a>, among many other contributions comprises many of the pillars upon which the field of <a href="/wiki/Distributed_computing" title="Distributed computing">distributed computing</a> is built. Shortly before his death in 2002, he received the <a href="/wiki/Association_for_Computing_Machinery" title="Association for Computing Machinery">ACM</a> <a href="/wiki/PODC_Influential-Paper_Award" class="mw-redirect" title="PODC Influential-Paper Award">PODC Influential-Paper Award</a> in distributed computing for his work on self-stabilization of program computation. This annual award was renamed the <a href="/wiki/Dijkstra_Prize" title="Dijkstra Prize">Dijkstra Prize</a> (<a href="/wiki/Edsger_W._Dijkstra_Prize_in_Distributed_Computing" class="mw-redirect" title="Edsger W. Dijkstra Prize in Distributed Computing">Edsger W. Dijkstra Prize in Distributed Computing</a>) the following year, in his honor.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>/n<p></p>/n<div id="toc" class="toc">/n<div class="toctitle">/n<h2>Contents</h2>/n</div>/n<ul>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Biography"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">Biography</span></a>/n<ul>/n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-2"><a href="#Early_years"><span class="tocnumber">1.1</span> <span class="toctext">Early years</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-3"><a href="#Mathematisch_Centrum.2C_Amsterdam"><span class="tocnumber">1.2</span> <span class="toctext">Mathematisch Centrum, Amsterdam</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-4"><a href="#Eindhoven_University_of_Technology"><span class="tocnumber">1.3</span> <span class="toctext">Eindhoven University of Technology</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-5"><a href="#Burroughs_Corporation"><span class="tocnumber">1.4</span> <span class="toctext">Burroughs Corporation</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-6"><a href="#The_University_of_Texas_at_Austin"><span class="tocnumber">1.5</span> <span class="toctext">The University of Texas at Austin</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-7"><a href="#Last_years"><span class="tocnumber">1.6</span> <span class="toctext">Last years</span></a></li>/n</ul>/n</li>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-8"><a href="#Scientific_contributions_and_impacts"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">Scientific contributions and impacts</span></a>/n<ul>/n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-9"><a href="#Algorithmic_work"><span class="tocnumber">2.1</span> <span class="toctext">Algorithmic work</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-10"><a href="#Compiler_construction_and_programming_language_research"><span class="tocnumber">2.2</span> <span class="toctext">Compiler construction and programming language research</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-11"><a href="#Programming_paradigm_and_methodology"><span class="tocnumber">2.3</span> <span class="toctext">Programming paradigm and methodology</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-12"><a href="#Program_design_and_development_.28software_engineering_research.29"><span class="tocnumber">2.4</span> <span class="toctext">Program design and development (software engineering research)</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-13"><a href="#Operating_system_research"><span class="tocnumber">2.5</span> <span class="toctext">Operating system research</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-14"><a href="#Concurrent_computing_and_programming"><span class="tocnumber">2.6</span> <span class="toctext">Concurrent computing and programming</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15"><a href="#Distributed_computing"><span class="tocnumber">2.7</span> <span class="toctext">Distributed computing</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-16"><a href="#Formal_specification_and_verification"><span class="tocnumber">2.8</span> <span class="toctext">Formal specification and verification</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-17"><a href="#On_the_nature_of_computer_science_and_computer_programming"><span class="tocnumber">2.9</span> <span class="toctext">On the nature of computer science and computer programming</span></a></li>/n</ul>/n</li>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-18"><a href="#Personality_and_working_style"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Personality and working style</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19"><a href="#EWDs"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">EWDs</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-20"><a href="#Personal_life"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Personal life</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21"><a href="#Death"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Death</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22"><a href="#Influence_and_recognition"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Influence and recognition</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23"><a href="#Awards_and_honors"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Awards and honors</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-25"><a href="#Publications"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Publications</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-26"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-27"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-28"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>/n</ul>/n</div>/n<p></p>/n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biography">Biography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Biography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>/n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_years">Early years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<p>Edsger W. Dijkstra was born in <a href="/wiki/Rotterdam" title="Rotterdam">Rotterdam</a>. His father was a chemist who was president of the Dutch Chemical Society; he taught chemistry at a secondary school and was later its superintendent. His mother was a mathematician, but never had a formal job.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>/n<p>Dijkstra had considered a career in law and had hoped to represent the Netherlands in the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>. However, after graduating from school in 1948, at his parents' suggestion he studied mathematics and physics and then <a href="/wiki/Theoretical_physics" title="Theoretical physics">theoretical physics</a> at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Leiden" class="mw-redirect" title="University of Leiden">University of Leiden</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Dijkstra_bio_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dijkstra_bio-10">[10]</a></sup></p>/n<p>In the early 1950s, <a href="/wiki/Electronic_computers" class="mw-redirect" title="Electronic computers">electronic computers</a> were a novelty. Dijkstra stumbled on his career quite by accident, and through his supervisor, Professor A. Haantjes, he met <a href="/wiki/Adriaan_van_Wijngaarden" title="Adriaan van Wijngaarden">Adriaan van Wijngaarden</a>, the director of the Computation Department at the <a href="/wiki/Centrum_Wiskunde_%26_Informatica" title="Centrum Wiskunde &amp; Informatica">Mathematical Center in Amsterdam</a>, who offered Dijkstra a job; he officially became the Netherlands' first "programmer" in March 1952.<sup id="cite_ref-Dijkstra_bio_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dijkstra_bio-10">[10]</a></sup></p>/n<p>For some time Dijkstra remained committed to physics, working on it in <a href="/wiki/Leiden" title="Leiden">Leiden</a> three days out of each week. With increasing exposure to computing, however, his focus began to shift. As he recalled:<sup id="cite_ref-ewd340_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ewd340-27">[27]</a></sup></p>/n<blockquote class="templatequote">/n<p>After having programmed for some three years, I had a discussion with <a href="/wiki/Adriaan_van_Wijngaarden" title="Adriaan van Wijngaarden">A. van Wijngaarden</a>, who was then my boss at the <a href="/wiki/Centrum_Wiskunde_%26_Informatica" title="Centrum Wiskunde &amp; Informatica">Mathematical Center in Amsterdam</a>, a discussion for which I shall remain grateful to him as long as I live. The point was that I was supposed to study theoretical physics at the University of Leiden simultaneously, and as I found the two activities harder and harder to combine, I had to make up my mind, either to stop programming and become a real, respectable <a href="/wiki/Theoretical_physicist" class="mw-redirect" title="Theoretical physicist">theoretical physicist</a>, or to carry my study of physics to a formal completion only, with a minimum of effort, and to become....., yes what? A <a href="/wiki/Programmer" title="Programmer">programmer</a>? But was that a respectable profession? For after all, what was <a href="/wiki/Computer_programming" title="Computer programming">programming</a>? Where was the sound body of knowledge that could support it as an intellectually respectable discipline? I remember quite vividly how I envied my hardware colleagues, who, when asked about their professional competence, could at least point out that they knew everything about vacuum tubes, amplifiers and the rest, whereas I felt that, when faced with that question, I would stand empty-handed. Full of misgivings I knocked on van Wijngaarden's office door, asking him whether I could "speak to him for a moment"; when I left his office a number of hours later, I was another person. For after having listened to my problems patiently, he agreed that up till that moment there was not much of a programming discipline, but then he went on to explain quietly that automatic computers were here to stay, that we were just at the beginning and could not I be one of the persons called to make programming a respectable discipline in the years to come? This was a turning point in my life and I completed my study of physics formally as quickly as I could.</p>/n<div class="templatequotecite">— <cite>Edsger Dijkstra, The Humble Programmer (EWD340), <a href="/wiki/Communications_of_the_ACM" title="Communications of the ACM">Communications of the ACM</a></cite></div>/n</blockquote>/n<p>When Dijkstra married Maria (Ria) C. Debets in 1957, he was required as a part of the marriage rites to state his profession. He stated that he was a programmer, which was unacceptable to the authorities, there being no such profession at that time in The Netherlands.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p>/n<p>In 1959 he received his PhD from the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Amsterdam" title="University of Amsterdam">University of Amsterdam</a> for a thesis entitled 'Communication with an Automatic Computer', devoted to a description of the assembly language designed for the first commercial computer developed in the Netherlands, the X1. His thesis supervisor was van Wijngaarden.<sup id="cite_ref-Apt.2C_Krzysztof_R._2002_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Apt.2C_Krzysztof_R._2002-12">[12]</a></sup></p>/n<h3><span id="Mathematisch_Centrum,_Amsterdam"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Mathematisch_Centrum.2C_Amsterdam">Mathematisch Centrum, Amsterdam</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Mathematisch Centrum, Amsterdam">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<div class="thumb tright">/n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Centrum-wiskunde-informatica-logo.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4d/Centrum-wiskunde-informatica-logo.png/180px-Centrum-wiskunde-informatica-logo.png" width="180" height="33" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4d/Centrum-wiskunde-informatica-logo.png/270px-Centrum-wiskunde-informatica-logo.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4d/Centrum-wiskunde-informatica-logo.png/360px-Centrum-wiskunde-informatica-logo.png 2x" data-file-width="442" data-file-height="82" /></a>/n<div class="thumbcaption">/n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Centrum-wiskunde-informatica-logo.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>/nThe <a href="/wiki/Centrum_Wiskunde_%26_Informatica" title="Centrum Wiskunde &amp; Informatica">CWI</a>'s logo. Dijkstra worked as a <a href="/wiki/Programmer" title="Programmer">programmer</a> at CWI (<a href="/wiki/Centrum_Wiskunde_%26_Informatica" title="Centrum Wiskunde &amp; Informatica">Centrum Wiskunde &amp; Informatica</a> / <a href="/wiki/Mathematisch_Centrum" class="mw-redirect" title="Mathematisch Centrum">Mathematisch Centrum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amsterdam" title="Amsterdam">Amsterdam</a>) from 1952 to 1962.</div>/n</div>/n</div>/n<p>From 1952 until 1962 Dijkstra worked at the <a href="/wiki/Mathematisch_Centrum" class="mw-redirect" title="Mathematisch Centrum">Mathematisch Centrum</a> in Amsterdam,<sup id="cite_ref-Apt.2C_Krzysztof_R._2002_12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Apt.2C_Krzysztof_R._2002-12">[12]</a></sup> where he worked closely with <a href="/wiki/Bram_Jan_Loopstra" title="Bram Jan Loopstra">Bram Jan Loopstra</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carel_S._Scholten" title="Carel S. Scholten">Carel S. Scholten</a>, who had been hired to build a computer. Their mode of interaction was disciplined: They would first decide upon the interface between the hardware and the software, by writing a programming manual. Then the hardware designers would have to be faithful to their part of the contract, while Dijkstra, the programmer, would write software for the nonexistent machine. Two of the lessons he learned from this experience were the importance of clear documentation, and that program debugging can be largely avoided through careful design.<sup id="cite_ref-Dijkstra_bio_10-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dijkstra_bio-10">[10]</a></sup> Dijkstra formulated and solved the <a href="/wiki/Shortest_path_problem" title="Shortest path problem">shortest path problem</a> for a demonstration at the official inauguration of the ARMAC computer in 1956, but—because of the absence of journals dedicated to automatic computing—did not publish the result until 1959.</p>/n<p>At the Mathematical Center, Dijkstra and his colleague <a href="/w/index.php?title=Jaap_Zonneveld&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Jaap Zonneveld (page does not exist)">Jaap Zonneveld</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;(<a href="https://nl.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jaap_Zonneveld" class="extiw" title="nl:Jaap Zonneveld">nl</a>)</span> developed a <a href="/wiki/Compiler" title="Compiler">compiler</a> for the programming language <a href="/wiki/ALGOL_60" title="ALGOL 60">ALGOL 60</a>; it had a profound influence on his later thinking on programming as a scientific activity. He and Zonneveld had completed the implementation of the first <a href="/wiki/ALGOL_60" title="ALGOL 60">ALGOL 60</a> <a href="/wiki/Compiler" title="Compiler">compiler</a> by August 1960, more than a year before a compiler was produced by another group.<sup id="cite_ref-Dijkstra_bio_10-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dijkstra_bio-10">[10]</a></sup></p>/n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eindhoven_University_of_Technology">Eindhoven University of Technology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Eindhoven University of Technology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<div class="thumb tright">/n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:TU_Eindhoven.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/TU_Eindhoven.jpg/180px-TU_Eindhoven.jpg" width="180" height="135" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/TU_Eindhoven.jpg/270px-TU_Eindhoven.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/TU_Eindhoven.jpg/360px-TU_Eindhoven.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600" /></a>/n<div class="thumbcaption">/n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:TU_Eindhoven.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>/nThe <a href="/wiki/Eindhoven_University_of_Technology" title="Eindhoven University of Technology">Eindhoven University of Technology</a>, located in <a href="/wiki/Eindhoven" title="Eindhoven">Eindhoven</a> in the south of the Netherlands, where Dijkstra was a professor of mathematics from 1962 to 1984.</div>/n</div>/n</div>/n<p>In 1962 Dijkstra moved to <a href="/wiki/Eindhoven" title="Eindhoven">Eindhoven</a>, and later to <a href="/wiki/Nuenen" title="Nuenen">Nuenen</a>, in the south of the Netherlands, where he became a professor in the Mathematics Department at the <a href="/wiki/Eindhoven_University_of_Technology" title="Eindhoven University of Technology">Eindhoven University of Technology</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Apt.2C_Krzysztof_R._2002_12-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Apt.2C_Krzysztof_R._2002-12">[12]</a></sup> The university did not have a separate computer science department and the culture of the mathematics department did not particularly suit him. Dijkstra tried to build a group of computer scientists who could collaborate on solving problems. This was an unusual model of research for the Mathematics Department.<sup id="cite_ref-Dijkstra_bio_10-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dijkstra_bio-10">[10]</a></sup> In the late 1960s he built the <a href="/wiki/THE_multiprogramming_system" title="THE multiprogramming system">THE operating system</a> (named for the university, then known as <a href="/wiki/Technische_Hogeschool_Eindhoven" class="mw-redirect" title="Technische Hogeschool Eindhoven">Technische Hogeschool Eindhoven</a>), which has influenced the designs of subsequent <a href="/wiki/Operating_systems" class="mw-redirect" title="Operating systems">operating systems</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>/n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Burroughs_Corporation">Burroughs Corporation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Burroughs Corporation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<p>Dijkstra joined <a href="/wiki/Burroughs_Corporation" title="Burroughs Corporation">Burroughs Corporation</a>, a company known at that time for the production of computers based on an innovative hardware architecture, as its Research Fellow in August 1973. His duties consisted of visiting some of the company's research centers a few times a year and carrying on his own research, which he did in the smallest Burroughs research facility, namely, his study on the second floor of his house in Nuenen. In fact, Dijkstra was the only research fellow of Burroughs Corporation and worked for it from home, occasionally travelling to its branches in the United States. As a result, he reduced his appointment at the university to one day a week. That day, Tuesday, soon became known as the day of the famous 'Tuesday Afternoon Club', a seminar during which he discussed with his colleagues scientific articles, looking at all aspects – notation, organisation, presentation, language, content, etc. Shortly after he moved in 1984 to the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Texas_at_Austin" title="University of Texas at Austin">University of Texas at Austin</a> (USA), a new 'branch' of the Tuesday Afternoon Club emerged in <a href="/wiki/Austin,_Texas" title="Austin, Texas">Austin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Apt.2C_Krzysztof_R._2002_12-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Apt.2C_Krzysztof_R._2002-12">[12]</a></sup></p>/n<p>The Burroughs years saw him at his most prolific in output of research articles. He wrote nearly 500 documents in the EWD series (described below), most of them technical reports, for private circulation within a select group.<sup id="cite_ref-Dijkstra_bio_10-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dijkstra_bio-10">[10]</a></sup></p>/n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_University_of_Texas_at_Austin">The University of Texas at Austin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: The University of Texas at Austin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<div class="thumb tright">/n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Main_Building_at_The_University_of_Texas_at_Austin.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Main_Building_at_The_University_of_Texas_at_Austin.jpg/180px-Main_Building_at_The_University_of_Texas_at_Austin.jpg" width="180" height="135" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Main_Building_at_The_University_of_Texas_at_Austin.jpg/270px-Main_Building_at_The_University_of_Texas_at_Austin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Main_Building_at_The_University_of_Texas_at_Austin.jpg/360px-Main_Building_at_The_University_of_Texas_at_Austin.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a>/n<div class="thumbcaption">/n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Main_Building_at_The_University_of_Texas_at_Austin.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>/nThe <a href="/wiki/University_of_Texas_at_Austin" title="University of Texas at Austin">University of Texas at Austin</a>, where Dijkstra held the Schlumberger Centennial Chair in Computer Sciences from 1984 until 1999.</div>/n</div>/n</div>/n<p>Dijkstra accepted the Schlumberger Centennial Chair in the Computer Science Department at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Texas_at_Austin" title="University of Texas at Austin">University of Texas at Austin</a> in 1984.</p>/n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Last_years">Last years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Last years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<p>Dijkstra worked in <a href="/wiki/Austin,_Texas" title="Austin, Texas">Austin</a> until his retirement in November 1999. To mark the occasion and to celebrate his forty-plus years of seminal contributions to <a href="/wiki/Computing_science" class="mw-redirect" title="Computing science">computing science</a>, the Department of Computer Sciences organized a symposium, which took place on his 70th birthday in May 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-Dijkstra_bio_10-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dijkstra_bio-10">[10]</a></sup></p>/n<p>Dijkstra and his wife returned from Austin to his original house in <a href="/wiki/Nuenen" title="Nuenen">Nuenen</a> (Netherlands) where he found that he had only months to live. He said that he wanted to retire in Austin, <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a>, but to die in the Netherlands. Dijkstra died on 6 August 2002 after a long struggle with cancer.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> He and his wife Maria (Ria) Debets were survived by their three children: Marcus, Femke and the computer scientist Rutger M. Dijkstra.</p>/n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Scientific_contributions_and_impacts">Scientific contributions and impacts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Scientific contributions and impacts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>/n<table class="vertical-navbox nowraplinks" style="float:right;clear:right;width:22.0em;margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em;background:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;padding:0.2em;border-spacing:0.4em 0;text-align:center;line-height:1.4em;font-size:88%">/n<tr>/n<th style="padding:0.2em 0.4em 0.2em;font-size:145%;line-height:1.2em"><a href="/wiki/Programming_paradigm" title="Programming paradigm">Programming paradigms</a></th>/n</tr>/n<tr>/n<td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em;text-align: left">/n<ul>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Action_language" title="Action language">Action</a></li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Agent-oriented_programming" title="Agent-oriented programming">Agent-oriented</a></li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Array_programming" title="Array programming">Array-oriented</a></li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Automata-based_programming" title="Automata-based programming">Automata-based</a></li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Concept_programming" title="Concept programming">Concept</a></li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Concurrent_computing" title="Concurrent computing">Concurrent computing</a>/n<ul>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Relativistic_programming" title="Relativistic programming">Relativistic programming</a></li>/n</ul>/n</li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Data-driven_programming" title="Data-driven programming">Data-driven</a></li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Declarative_programming" title="Declarative programming">Declarative</a> (contrast: <a href="/wiki/Imperative_programming" title="Imperative programming">Flow-based</a></li>/n<li>Cell-oriented (<a href="/wiki/Spreadsheet" title="Spreadsheet">spreadsheets</a>)</li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Reactive_programming" title="Reactive programming">Inductive logic</a></li>/n</ul>/n</li>/n</ul>/n</li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Dynamic_programming_language" title="Dynamic programming language">Dynamic</a></li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/End-user_development" title="End-user development">End-user programming</a></li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Event-driven_programming" title="Event-driven programming">Event-driven</a>/n<ul>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Service-oriented_architecture" title="Service-oriented architecture">Service-oriented</a></li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Time-driven_programming" title="Time-driven programming">Time-driven</a></li>/n</ul>/n</li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Expression-oriented_programming_language" title="Expression-oriented programming language">Expression-oriented</a></li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Feature-oriented_programming" title="Feature-oriented programming">Feature-oriented</a></li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Function-level_programming" title="Function-level programming">Function-level</a> (contrast: <a href="/wiki/Value-level_programming" title="Value-level programming">Value-level</a>)</li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Generic_programming" title="Generic programming">Generic</a></li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Imperative_programming" title="Imperative programming">Imperative</a> (contrast: <a href="/wiki/Declarative_programming" title="Declarative programming">Declarative</a>)/n<ul>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Literate_programming" title="Literate programming">Literate</a></li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Procedural_programming" (computing)">Dialecting</a></li>/n</ul>/n</li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Intentional_programming" title="Intentional programming">Intentional</a></li>/n</ul>/n</li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Metaprogramming" title="Inductive programming">Inductive programming</a></li>/n</ul>/n</li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Reflection_(computer_programming)" title="Reflection (computer programming)">Reflective</a>/n<ul>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Attribute-oriented_programming" title="Attribute-oriented programming">Attribute-oriented</a></li>/n</ul>/n</li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Homoiconicity" title="Homoiconicity">Homoiconic</a></li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Macro_(computer_science)" title="Macro (computer science)">Macro</a></li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Template_metaprogramming" title="Template metaprogramming">Template</a>/n<ul>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Policy-based_design" title="Policy-based design">Policy-based</a></li>/n</ul>/n</li>/n</ul>/n</li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Non-structured_programming" title="Non-structured programming">Non-structured</a> (contrast: <a href="/wiki/Structured_programming" title="Structured programming">Structured</a>)/n<ul>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Array_programming" title="Array programming">Array</a></li>/n</ul>/n</li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Nondeterministic_programming" title="Nondeterministic programming">Nondeterministic</a></li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Parallel_computing" title="Parallel computing">Parallel computing</a>/n<ul>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Process-oriented_programming" title="Process-oriented programming">Process-oriented</a></li>/n</ul>/n</li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Tacit_programming" title="Tacit programming">Point-free style</a>/n<ul>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Concatenative_programming_language" title="Concatenative programming language">Concatenative</a></li>/n</ul>/n</li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Probabilistic_programming_language" title="Probabilistic programming language">Probabilistic</a></li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Semantic-oriented_programming" title="Semantic-oriented programming">Semantic</a></li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Structured_programming" title="Structured programming">Structured</a> (contrast: <a href="/wiki/Non-structured_programming" title="Non-structured programming">Non-structured</a>)/n<ul>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Block_(programming)" title="Block (programming)">Block-structured</a></li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Modular_programming" title="Modular programming">Modular</a> (contrast: <a href="/wiki/Monolithic_application" title="Monolithic application">Monolithic</a>)</li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Object-oriented_programming" title="Object-oriented programming">Object-oriented</a>/n<ul>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Actor_model" title="Actor model">Actor-based</a></li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Class-based_programming" title="Class-based programming">Class-based</a></li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Concurrent_object-oriented_programming" title="Concurrent object-oriented programming">Concurrent</a></li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Prototype-based_programming" title="Prototype-based programming">Prototype-based</a></li>/n<li>By <a href="/wiki/Separation_of_concerns" title="Separation of concerns">separation of concerns</a>:/n<ul>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Aspect-oriented_programming" title="Aspect-oriented programming">Aspect-oriented</a></li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Role-oriented_programming" title="Role-oriented programming">Role-oriented</a></li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Subject-oriented_programming" title="Subject-oriented programming">Subject-oriented</a></li>/n</ul>/n</li>/n</ul>/n</li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Recursion_(computer_science)" title="Recursion (computer science)">Recursive</a></li>/n</ul>/n</li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Value-level_programming" title="Value-level programming">Value-level</a> (contrast: <a href="/wiki/Function-level_programming" title="Function-level programming">Function-level</a>)</li>/n</ul>/n</td>/n</tr>/n<tr>/n<td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%">/n<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">/n<ul>/n<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Programming_paradigms" title="Template:Programming paradigms"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>/n<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Programming_paradigms" title="Template talk:Programming paradigms"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>/n<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Programming_paradigms&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>/n</ul>/n</div>/n</td>/n</tr>/n</table>/n<p>As an early theoretical <a href="/wiki/List_of_pioneers_in_computer_science" title="List of pioneers in computer science">pioneer</a> in many research areas of computing science, Dijkstra helped shape the <a href="/wiki/List_of_pioneers_in_computer_science" title="List of pioneers in computer science">new discipline</a> from both an engineering and an <a href="/wiki/Theoretical_computer_science" title="Theoretical computer science">academic perspective</a>. Many of his papers are the source of new research areas. Many concepts that are now standard in computer science were first identified by Dijkstra or bear names coined by him. Several important problems were also first formulated and solved by him. A 1994 survey of over a thousand professors of computer science was conducted to obtain a list of 38 most influential scholarly papers in the field, and Dijkstra is the author of five papers.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>/n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Algorithmic_work">Algorithmic work</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Algorithmic work">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<div class="thumb tright">/n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dijkstra_Animation.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Dijkstra_Animation.gif/220px-Dijkstra_Animation.gif" width="220" height="173" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/57/Dijkstra_Animation.gif 1.5x" data-file-width="283" data-file-height="222" /></a>/n<div class="thumbcaption">/n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dijkstra_Animation.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>/nDijkstra's algorithm. It picks the unvisited vertex with the lowest-distance, calculates the distance through it to each unvisited neighbor, and updates the neighbor's distance if smaller. Mark visited (set to red) when done with neighbors.</div>/n</div>/n</div>/n<p>Dijkstra's algorithmic work (especially <a href="/wiki/Graph_algorithms" class="mw-redirect" title="Graph algorithms">graph algorithms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Concurrent_algorithm" title="Concurrent algorithm">concurrent algorithms</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Distributed_algorithms" class="mw-redirect" title="Distributed algorithms">distributed algorithms</a>) plays an important role in many areas of computing science. According to <a href="/wiki/Leslie_Lamport" title="Leslie Lamport">Leslie Lamport</a> (2002), Dijkstra "started the field of concurrent and <a href="/wiki/Distributed_algorithms" class="mw-redirect" title="Distributed algorithms">distributed algorithms</a> with his 1965 CACM paper "Solution of a Problem in Concurrent Programming Control", in which he first stated and solved the mutual exclusion problem." As Lamport explains, "that paper is probably why <a href="/wiki/PODC" class="mw-redirect" title="PODC">PODC</a> exists (...). It remains to this day the most influential paper in the field. That it did not win a <a href="/wiki/PODC_Influential-Paper_Award" class="mw-redirect" title="PODC Influential-Paper Award">PODC Influential Paper Award</a> reflects an artificial separation between concurrent and distributed algorithms–a separation that has never existed in Dijkstra's work."<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>/n<p>In 1959 Dijkstra published in a 3-page article 'A note on two problems in connexion with graphs' the <a href="/wiki/Algorithm" title="Algorithm">algorithm</a> to find the shortest path in a graph between any two given nodes, now called <a href="/wiki/Dijkstra%27s_algorithm" title="Dijkstra's algorithm">Dijkstra's algorithm</a>. Its impact over the next 40 years is summarised from the article of <a href="/wiki/Mikkel_Thorup" title="Mikkel Thorup">Mikkel Thorup</a>, 'Undirected Single Source Shortest Paths with Positive Integer Weights in Linear Time' (1999): "Since 1959, all theoretical developments in SSSP [Single-Source Shortest Paths] for general directed and undirected graphs have been based on Dijkstra's algorithm." Dijkstra's algorithm is used in SPF, <a href="/wiki/Shortest_Path_First" class="mw-redirect" title="Shortest Path First">Shortest Path First</a>, which is used in the routing protocols <a href="/wiki/Open_Shortest_Path_First" title="Open Shortest Path First">OSPF</a> and <a href="/wiki/IS-IS" title="IS-IS">IS-IS</a>. Various modifications to Dijkstra's algorithm have been proposed by many authors using <a href="/wiki/Heuristic_(computer_science)" title="Heuristic (computer science)">heuristics</a> to reduce the run time of <a href="/w/index.php?title=Shortest_path_search&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Shortest path search (page does not exist)">shortest path search</a>. One of the most used heuristic algorithms is the <a href="/wiki/A*_search_algorithm" title="A* search algorithm">A* search algorithm</a> (first described by <a href="/wiki/Peter_E._Hart" title="Peter E. Hart">Peter Hart</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nils_John_Nilsson" title="Nils John Nilsson">Nils Nilsson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bertram_Raphael" title="Bertram Raphael">Bertram Raphael</a> of <a href="/wiki/SRI_International" title="SRI International">Stanford Research Institute</a> in 1968),<sup id="cite_ref-nilsson_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nilsson-35">[35]</a></sup> the main goal is to reduce the run time by reducing the search space. Dijkstra thought about the <a href="/wiki/Shortest_path_problem" title="Shortest path problem">shortest path problem</a> when working at the <a href="/wiki/Centrum_Wiskunde_%26_Informatica" title="Centrum Wiskunde &amp; Informatica">Mathematical Center in Amsterdam</a> in 1956 as a <a href="/wiki/Computer_programmer" class="mw-redirect" title="Computer programmer">programmer</a> to demonstrate capabilities of a new <a href="/wiki/Computer" title="Computer">computer</a> called <a href="/w/index.php?title=ARMAC&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="ARMAC (page does not exist)">ARMAC</a>. His objective was to choose both a problem as well as an answer (that would be produced by computer) that non-computing people could understand. He designed the <a href="/wiki/Dijkstra%27s_algorithm" title="Dijkstra's algorithm">shortest path algorithm</a> in about 20 minutes without aid of paper and pen and later implemented it for ARMAC for a slightly simplified transportation map of 64 cities in the Netherlands (so that 6 bits would suffice to represent the city in the algorithm).<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></p>/n<p>A year later, he came across another problem from hardware engineers working on the institute's next computer: minimize the amount of wire needed to connect the pins on the back panel of the machine. As a solution, he re-discovered the algorithm known as <a href="/wiki/Prim%27s_algorithm" title="Prim's algorithm">Prim's</a> <a href="/wiki/Minimum_spanning_tree" title="Minimum spanning tree">minimal spanning tree algorithm</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Prim%27s_algorithm" title="Prim's algorithm">Prim's algorithm</a> was originally developed in 1930 by <a href="/wiki/Czech_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Czech people">Czech</a> mathematician <a href="/wiki/Vojt%C4%9Bch_Jarn%C3%ADk" title="Vojtěch Jarník">Vojtěch Jarník</a><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> and later independently rediscovered and republished by <a href="/wiki/Robert_C._Prim" title="Robert C. Prim">Robert C. Prim</a> in 1957<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> and Dijkstra in 1959.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Therefore, it is also sometimes called the <a href="/wiki/DJP_algorithm" class="mw-redirect" title="DJP algorithm">DJP algorithm</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-pr02_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pr02-41">[41]</a></sup></p>/n<p>In 1961 Dijkstra first described the <a href="/wiki/Shunting-yard_algorithm" title="Shunting-yard algorithm">shunting-yard algorithm</a>, a method for parsing mathematical expressions specified in <a href="/wiki/Infix_notation" title="Infix notation">infix notation</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Mathematisch_Centrum" class="mw-redirect" title="Mathematisch Centrum">Mathematisch Centrum</a> report.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> It can be used to produce output in <a href="/wiki/Reverse_Polish_notation" title="Reverse Polish notation">Reverse Polish notation</a> (RPN) or as an <a href="/wiki/Abstract_syntax_tree" title="Abstract syntax tree">abstract syntax tree</a> (AST). The <a href="/wiki/Algorithm" title="Algorithm">algorithm</a> was named the "shunting yard" algorithm because its operation resembles that of a <a href="/wiki/Classification_yard" title="Classification yard">railroad shunting yard</a>. The shunting-yard algorithm is commonly used to implement <a href="/wiki/Operator-precedence_parser" title="Operator-precedence parser">operator-precedence parsers</a>.</p>/n<p>In 1962 or 1963 Dijkstra proposed the <a href="/wiki/Semaphore_(programming)" title="Semaphore (programming)">semaphore</a> mechanism for <a href="/wiki/Mutual_exclusion" title="Mutual exclusion">mutual exclusion</a> algorithm for n processes (a generalization of <a href="/wiki/Dekker%27s_algorithm" title="Dekker's algorithm">Dekker's algorithm</a>), which was probably the first published <a href="/wiki/Concurrent_algorithm" title="Concurrent algorithm">concurrent algorithm</a> and which introduced a new area of algorithmic research. He also identified the <a href="/wiki/Deadlock" title="Deadlock">deadlock</a> problem and proposed the <a href="/wiki/Banker%27s_algorithm" title="Banker's algorithm">banker's algorithm</a> that <a href="/wiki/Deadlock_prevention_algorithms" title="Deadlock prevention algorithms">prevents deadlock</a>.</p>/n<p>In 1974 Dijkstra presented three <a href="/wiki/Self-stabilization" title="Self-stabilization">self-stabilizing</a> algorithms for <a href="/wiki/Mutual_exclusion" title="Mutual exclusion">mutual exclusion</a> on a ring. Dijkstra's work is considered to be the first to introduce and demonstrate the <a href="/wiki/Self-stabilization" title="Self-stabilization">self-stabilization</a> concept.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>/n<p>In the mid-1970s Dijkstra (together with other authors) introduced two useful abstractions (mutator and collector) to the study of <a href="/wiki/Garbage_collection_(computer_science)" title="Garbage collection (computer science)">garbage collection</a>. The mutator abstracts the process that performs the computation, including allocation of a new storage cell. The collector is the process that automatically reclaims garbage. Furthermore, this paper gives a formalization of "<a href="/wiki/Tracing_garbage_collection#Tri-color_marking" title="Tracing garbage collection">tri-color marking</a>" that is basic to incremental garbage collection.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></p>/n<p>In the early 1980s Dijkstra and <a href="/wiki/Carel_S._Scholten" title="Carel S. Scholten">Carel S. Scholten</a> proposed the <a href="/wiki/Dijkstra%E2%80%93Scholten_algorithm" title="Dijkstra–Scholten algorithm">Dijkstra–Scholten algorithm</a> for detecting termination in <a href="/wiki/Distributed_systems" class="mw-redirect" title="Distributed systems">distributed systems</a>.</p>/n<p>In 1981 Dijkstra developed <a href="/wiki/Smoothsort" title="Smoothsort">smoothsort</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Comparison_sort" title="Comparison sort">comparison-based</a> <a href="/wiki/Sorting_algorithm" title="Sorting algorithm">sorting algorithm</a> and a variation of <a href="/wiki/Heapsort" title="Heapsort">heapsort</a>.</p>/n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Compiler_construction_and_programming_language_research">Compiler construction and programming language research</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Compiler construction and programming language research">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<div class="thumb tright">/n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Detail_of_a_1Kb_ferrite_core_RAM-module_of_an_1960s_Electrologica_X1_computer.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Detail_of_a_1Kb_ferrite_core_RAM-module_of_an_1960s_Electrologica_X1_computer.jpg/180px-Detail_of_a_1Kb_ferrite_core_RAM-module_of_an_1960s_Electrologica_X1_computer.jpg" width="180" height="135" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Detail_of_a_1Kb_ferrite_core_RAM-module_of_an_1960s_Electrologica_X1_computer.jpg/270px-Detail_of_a_1Kb_ferrite_core_RAM-module_of_an_1960s_Electrologica_X1_computer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Detail_of_a_1Kb_ferrite_core_RAM-module_of_an_1960s_Electrologica_X1_computer.jpg/360px-Detail_of_a_1Kb_ferrite_core_RAM-module_of_an_1960s_Electrologica_X1_computer.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a>/n<div class="thumbcaption">/n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Detail_of_a_1Kb_ferrite_core_RAM-module_of_an_1960s_Electrologica_X1_computer.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>/nBy August 1960, Dijkstra and his colleague Zonneveld had completed the <a href="/wiki/ALGOL_60#ALGOL_60_implementations_timeline" title="ALGOL 60">implementation of the first ALGOL 60 compiler</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Electrologica_X1" title="Electrologica X1">Electrologica X1</a> computer.</div>/n</div>/n</div>/n<p>Dijkstra was known to be a fan of <a href="/wiki/ALGOL" title="ALGOL">ALGOL 60</a>, and worked on the team that implemented the first <a href="/wiki/Compiler" title="Compiler">compiler</a> for that language. He was closely involved in the ALGOL 60 development, realisation and popularisation. As discussed by <a href="/wiki/Peter_Naur" title="Peter Naur">Peter Naur</a> in the article 'The European side of the last phase of the development of ALGOL 60', in the <i>Proceedings of the First ACM SIGPLAN Conference on History of Programming Languages</i>, January 1978, Dijkstra took part in the period 1958–1959 in a number of meetings that culminated in the publication of the report defining the ALGOL 60 language. Dijkstra's name does not appear in the list of 13 authors of the final report. Apparently, he eventually left the committee because he could not agree with the majority opinions. Still, while at the Mathematisch Centrum (Amsterdam), he wrote jointly with <a href="/w/index.php?title=Jaap_Zonneveld&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Jaap Zonneveld (page does not exist)">Jaap Zonneveld</a> the <a href="/wiki/ALGOL_60#ALGOL_60_implementations_timeline" title="ALGOL 60">first ALGOL 60 compiler</a>. Dijkstra and Zonneveld, who collaborated on the compiler, agreed not to shave until the project was completed; while Zonneveld shaved shortly thereafter, Dijkstra kept his beard for the rest of his life.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p>/n<p><a href="/wiki/ALGOL" title="ALGOL">ALGOL</a> was the result of a collaboration of American and European committees. <a href="/wiki/ALGOL_60" title="ALGOL 60">ALGOL 60</a> (short for ALGOrithmic Language 1960) is a member of the ALGOL family of computer programming languages. It followed on from <a href="/wiki/ALGOL_58" title="ALGOL 58">ALGOL 58</a> and inspired many languages that followed it. It gave rise to many other programming languages, including <a href="/wiki/BCPL" title="BCPL">BCPL</a>, <a href="/wiki/B_(programming_language)" title="B (programming language)">B</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pascal_(programming_language)" title="Pascal (programming language)">Pascal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Simula" title="Simula">Simula</a> and <a href="/wiki/C_(programming_language)" title="C (programming language)">C</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> Algol 60 was a sophisticatedly designed <a href="/wiki/Computer_language" title="Computer language">computer language</a> and it provided a large number of hitherto unknown <a href="/wiki/Programming_language_implementation" title="Programming language implementation">implementation</a> challenges. As <a href="/wiki/Bjarne_Stroustrup" title="Bjarne Stroustrup">Bjarne Stroustrup</a> notes, "one problem with Algol60 was that no one knew how to implement it."<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> A major new challenge in <a href="/wiki/ALGOL_60#ALGOL_60_implementations_timeline" title="ALGOL 60">Algol 60 implementation</a> was the run-time allocation and management of data. In 1960 Dijkstra and Zonneveld showed how recursive procedures could be executed using a run-time stack of activation records, and how to efficiently access identifiers from statically enclosing scopes using the so-called 'display'.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> The ALGOL 60 compiler was one of the first to support <a href="/wiki/Recursion_(computer_science)" title="Recursion (computer science)">recursion</a><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> employing a novel method to do so. Dijkstra's short book <i>Primer of Algol 60 Programming</i>, originally published in 1962, was the standard reference for the language for several years.</p>/n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Programming_paradigm_and_methodology">Programming paradigm and methodology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Programming paradigm and methodology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<p><a href="/wiki/Computer_programming" title="Computer programming">Computer programming</a> in the 1950s to 1960s was not recognized as an <a href="/wiki/Discipline_(academia)" title="Discipline (academia)">academic discipline</a> and unlike mature sciences there were no theoretical concepts or coding systems. Programming as a professional activity was poorly understood in those years.</p>/n<p>In the late 1960s computer programming was in state of crisis. <a href="/wiki/Software_crisis" title="Software crisis">Software crisis</a> is a term used in the early days of <a href="/wiki/Computing_science" class="mw-redirect" title="Computing science">computing science</a> for the difficulty of writing useful and efficient <a href="/wiki/Computer_programs" class="mw-redirect" title="Computer programs">computer programs</a> in the required time. The software crisis was due to the rapid increases in computer power and the complexity of the problems that could be tackled. With the increase in the complexity of the <a href="/wiki/Software" title="Software">software</a>, many software problems arose because existing methods were neither sufficient nor up to the mark. The term "software crisis" was coined by some attendees at the first <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a> Software Engineering Conference in 1968 at <a href="/wiki/Garmisch" class="mw-redirect" title="Garmisch">Garmisch</a>, Germany.<sup id="cite_ref-Haigh.2C_Thomas_2010_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haigh.2C_Thomas_2010-51">[51]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nato_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nato-52">[52]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nato2_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nato2-53">[53]</a></sup> His 1972 ACM Turing Award Lecture makes reference to this same problem: "The major cause of the software crisis is that the machines have become several orders of magnitude more powerful! To put it quite bluntly: as long as there were no machines, programming was no problem at all; when we had a few weak computers, programming became a mild problem, and now we have gigantic computers, programming has become an equally gigantic problem."<sup id="cite_ref-ewd340_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ewd340-27">[27]</a></sup></p>/n<p>While Dijkstra had programmed extensively in machine code in the 1950s, he came to the conclusion that in high-level languages frequent use of the <a href="/wiki/GOTO" class="mw-redirect" title="GOTO">GOTO</a> statement was usually symptomatic of poor structure. In 1968 he wrote a private paper "A Case against the GO TO Statement",<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> which was then published as a letter in CACM.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Editor <a href="/wiki/Niklaus_Wirth" title="Niklaus Wirth">Niklaus Wirth</a> gave this letter the heading "<a href="/wiki/Go_To_Statement_Considered_Harmful" class="mw-redirect" title="Go To Statement Considered Harmful">Go To Statement Considered Harmful</a>", which introduced the phrase "<a href="/wiki/Considered_harmful" title="Considered harmful">considered harmful</a>" into computing.</p>/n<p>Dijkstra argued that the programming statement GOTO, found in many high-level programming languages, is a major source of errors, and should therefore be eliminated. This letter caused a huge debate in the programming community. Some went to the length of equating good programming with the elimination of <a href="/wiki/GO_TO" class="mw-redirect" title="GO TO">GO TO</a>. Dijkstra refused to mention the debate, or even the GO TO statement, in his article "Notes on Structured Programming". The debate has long since died down; programming languages provide alternatives to the GO TO, few programmers today use it liberally, and most never use it at all.<sup id="cite_ref-Dijkstra_bio_10-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dijkstra_bio-10">[10]</a></sup></p>/n<p>Dijkstra's thesis was that departures from linear control flow were clearer if allowed only in disciplined higher-level structures such as the <a href="/wiki/Conditional_(computer_programming)" title="Conditional (computer programming)">if-then-else</a> statement and the <a href="/wiki/While_loop" title="While loop">while loop</a>. This methodology was developed into <a href="/wiki/Structured_programming" title="Structured programming">structured programming movement</a>, the title of his 1972 book, coauthored with <a href="/wiki/C.A.R._Hoare" class="mw-redirect" title="C.A.R. Hoare">C.A.R. Hoare</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ole-Johan_Dahl" title="Ole-Johan Dahl">Ole-Johan Dahl</a>. Considered by many as the first significant movement in history of computer programming, structured programming became the new programming orthodoxy during the 1970s.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bertrand_Meyer" title="Bertrand Meyer">Bertrand Meyer</a> remarked that, "The revolution in views of programming started by Dijkstra's iconoclasm led to a movement known as structured programming, which advocated a systematic, rational approach to program construction. Structured programming is the basis for all that has been done since in <a href="/wiki/Programming_methodology" class="mw-redirect" title="Programming methodology">programming methodology</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Object-oriented_programming" title="Object-oriented programming">object-oriented programming</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></p>/n<p><a href="/wiki/Structured_programming" title="Structured programming">Structured programming</a> is often regarded as "goto-less programming". But as Bertrand Meyer notes, "As the first book on the topic [<i>Structured Programming</i> by Dijkstra, Dahl, and Hoare] shows, structured programming is about much more than control structures and the <a href="/wiki/Goto" title="Goto">goto</a>. Its principal message is that programming should be considered a scientific discipline based on mathematical rigor."<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Category:Structured_programming_languages" title="Category:Structured programming languages">As a programming paradigm</a>, structured programming – especially in the 1970s and 1980s – significantly influenced the birth of many modern <a href="/wiki/Programming_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Programming languages">programming languages</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Pascal_(programming_language)" title="Pascal (programming language)">Pascal</a>, <a href="/wiki/C_(programming_language)" title="C (programming language)">C</a>, <a href="/wiki/Modula-2" title="Modula-2">Modula-2</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ada_(programming_language)" title="Ada (programming language)">Ada</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Fortran_77" class="mw-redirect" title="Fortran 77">Fortran 77</a> version which incorporates the concepts of structured programming, was released in 1978. The <a href="/wiki/C%2B%2B" title="C++">C++</a> language was a considerably extended and enhanced version of the popular <a href="/wiki/Category:Structured_programming_languages" title="Category:Structured programming languages">structured programming language</a> <a href="/wiki/C_(programming_language)" title="C (programming language)">C</a> (see also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_C-based_programming_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="List of C-based programming languages">list of C-based programming languages</a>). Since C++ was developed from a more traditional <a href="/wiki/Category:Structured_programming_languages" title="Category:Structured programming languages">structured language</a>, it is a '<a href="/wiki/Multi-paradigm_programming_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Multi-paradigm programming language">hybrid language</a>', rather than a pure <a href="/wiki/Object-oriented_programming" title="Object-oriented programming">object-oriented programming</a> language.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></p>/n<h3><span id="Program_design_and_development_(software_engineering_research)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Program_design_and_development_.28software_engineering_research.29">Program design and development (software engineering research)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Program design and development (software engineering research)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<p>Dijkstra's ideas about <a href="/wiki/Programming_methodology" class="mw-redirect" title="Programming methodology">programming methodology</a> (especially the structured programming movement) helped lay the foundations for the birth and development of the professional discipline of <a href="/wiki/Software_engineering" title="Software engineering">software engineering</a> (in particular the software design and development), enabling programmers to organize and manage increasingly complex software projects.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> In the late 1960s Dijkstra discussed the concept of program families. And in the mid 1970s <a href="/wiki/David_Parnas" title="David Parnas">David Parnas</a> and others clarified the idea and showed how to apply it in software engineering principles.</p>/n<p>The rise of the <a href="/wiki/Structured_programming" title="Structured programming">structured programming movement</a> led to many other <a href="/wiki/Structured_analysis" title="Structured analysis"><i>structured</i> approaches</a> applied to <a href="/wiki/Software_design" title="Software design">software design</a>. The techniques of <a href="/wiki/Structured_analysis" title="Structured analysis">structured analysis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Structured_design" class="mw-redirect" title="Structured design">structured design</a> are outgrowths of <a href="/wiki/Structured_programming" title="Structured programming">structured programming</a> concepts and techniques, and of the early ideas about modular design. Principles of modularity were strengthened by <a href="/wiki/Larry_Constantine" title="Larry Constantine">Larry Constantine</a>'s concepts of <a href="/wiki/Coupling_(computer_programming)" title="Coupling (computer programming)">coupling</a> (to be minimized between modules) and <a href="/wiki/Cohesion_(computer_science)" title="Cohesion (computer science)">cohesion</a> (to be maximized within modules), by <a href="/wiki/David_Parnas" title="David Parnas">David Parnas</a>'s techniques of <a href="/wiki/Information_hiding" title="Information hiding">information hiding</a>, and by <a href="/wiki/Abstract_data_types" class="mw-redirect" title="Abstract data types">abstract data types</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> A number of tools and methods employing <a href="/wiki/Structured_analysis" title="Structured analysis">structured concepts</a> were developed, such as <a href="/wiki/Structured_Design" class="mw-redirect" title="Structured Design">Structured Design</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jackson_Structured_Programming" class="mw-redirect" title="Jackson Structured Programming">Jackson's Structured Programming</a>, <a href="/wiki/Douglas_T._Ross" title="Douglas T. Ross">Ross</a>' <a href="/wiki/Structured_Analysis_and_Design_Technique" class="mw-redirect" title="Structured Analysis and Design Technique">Structured Analysis and Design Technique</a> (SADT), <a href="/wiki/Edward_Yourdon#Yourdon_Structured_Method" title="Edward Yourdon">Yourdon's Structured Method</a>, <a href="/wiki/Structured_Systems_Analysis_and_Design_Method" class="mw-redirect" title="Structured Systems Analysis and Design Method">Structured Systems Analysis and Design Method</a> (SSADM), and James Martin's <a href="/wiki/Information_Engineering" class="mw-redirect" title="Information Engineering">Information Engineering</a>. The field of <a href="/wiki/Software_metrics" class="mw-redirect" title="Software metrics">software metrics</a> is often considered as a direct influence of the structured programming movement on <a href="/wiki/Software_engineering" title="Software engineering">software engineering</a> in the 1970s.</p>/n<p><a href="/wiki/Separation_of_concerns" title="Separation of concerns">Separation of concerns</a> (SoC), one of the basic principles in <a href="/wiki/Software_engineering" title="Software engineering">software engineering</a>, is a design principle for separating a <a href="/wiki/Computer_program" title="Computer program">computer program</a> into distinct sections, such that each section addresses a separate <a href="/wiki/Concern_(computer_science)" title="Concern (computer science)">concern</a>. The term <i><a href="/wiki/Separation_of_concerns" title="Separation of concerns">separation of concerns</a></i> was coined by Dijkstra in his 1974 paper "On the role of scientific thought".<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>/n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Operating_system_research">Operating system research</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Operating system research">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<p>In the 1960s Dijkstra and his colleagues in <a href="/wiki/Eindhoven" title="Eindhoven">Eindhoven</a> designed and implemented <a href="/wiki/THE_multiprogramming_system" title="THE multiprogramming system">THE</a> (standing for '<a href="/wiki/Technische_Hogeschool_Eindhoven" class="mw-redirect" title="Technische Hogeschool Eindhoven">Technische Hogeschool Eindhoven</a>') <a href="/wiki/Operating_system" title="Operating system">operating system</a>, which was organised into clearly identified <a href="/wiki/Abstraction_layer" title="Abstraction layer">layers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> His 1968 article on this subject provided the foundation for subsequent designs of the operating systems.</p>/n<p>Dijkstra organized the design of the system in layers in order to reduce the overall complexity of the software. Though the term 'architecture' had not yet been used to describe <a href="/wiki/Software_design" title="Software design">software design</a>, this was certainly considered the first glimpse of <a href="/wiki/Software_architecture" title="Software architecture">software architecture</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> It introduced a number of design principles which have become part of the working vocabulary of every professional programmer: levels of abstraction, programming in layers, the semaphore, and <a href="/wiki/Cooperating_sequential_processes" class="mw-redirect" title="Cooperating sequential processes">cooperating sequential processes</a>. His original paper on the THE operating system was reprinted in the 25th Anniversary issue of Communications of the ACM, in January 1983. By way of introduction, the Editor-in-Chief says, "This project initiated a long line of research in multilevel systems architecture — a line that continues to the present day because hierarchical modularity is a powerful approach to organizing large systems."<sup id="cite_ref-Dijkstra_bio_10-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dijkstra_bio-10">[10]</a></sup></p>/n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Concurrent_computing_and_programming">Concurrent computing and programming</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edsger_W._Dijkstra&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Concurrent computing and programming">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<div class="thumb tright">/n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rail-semaphore-signal-Dave-F.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Rail-semaphore-signal-Dave-F.jpg/180px-Rail-semaphore-signal-Dave-F.jpg" width="180" height="270" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Rail-semaphore-signal-Dave-F.jpg/270px-Rail-semaphore-signal-Dave-F.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Rail-semaphore-signal-Dave-F.jpg/360px-Rail-semaphore-signal-Dave-F.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="3072" /></a>/n<div class="thumbcaption">/n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rail-semaphore-signal-Dave-F.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>/nA <a href="/wiki/Semaphore_(programming)" title="Semaphore (programming)">semaphore</a> (<a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch</a>: <span lang="nl" xml:lang="nl"><i>seinpaal</i></span>, the term used in Dijkstra's original description<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>). In the early 1960s Dijkstra proposed the first synchronisation mechanism for concurrent processes, the <a href="/wiki/Semaphore_(programming)" title="Semaphore (programming)">semaphore</a> with its two operations, P and V.</div>/n</div>/n</div>/n<p>In a one-page paper from 1965 Dijkstra introduced the '<a href="/wiki/Mutual_exclusion" title="Mutual exclusion">mutual exclusion</a> problem' for n processes and discussed a solution to it. It was probably the first published <a href="/wiki/Concurrent_algorithm" title="Concurrent algorithm">concurrent algorithm</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Lamport_.282015.29_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lamport_.282015.29-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Apt.2C_Krzysztof_R._2002_12-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Apt.2C_Krzysztof_R._2002-12">[12]</a></sup> The notion, standard by now, of a '<a href="/wiki/Critical_section" title="Critical section">critical section</a>' was also introduced in this paper. <a href="/wiki/Per_Brinch_Hansen" title="Per Brinch Hansen">Per Brinch Hansen</a>, a pioneer in the field of <a href="/wiki/Concurrent_computing" title="Concurrent computing">concurrent computing</a>, considers Dijkstra's <i>Cooperating Sequential Processes</i> (1965) to be the <a href="/wiki/List_of_important_publications_in_concurrent,_parallel,_and_distributed_computing" title="List of important publications in concurrent, parallel, and distributed computing">first classic paper</a> in concurrent programming. As Brinch Hansen notes, 'Dijkstra lays the conceptual foundation for abstract concurrent programming' with that paper.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>/n<div class="thumb tright">/n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mutual_exclusion_example_with_linked_list.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Mutual_exclusion_example_with_linked_list.png/180px-Mutual_exclusion_example_with_linked_list.png" width="180" height="128" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Mutual_exclusion_example_with_linked_list.png/270px-Mutual_exclusion_example_with_linked_list.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Mutual_exclusion_example_with_linked_list.png/360px-Mutual_exclusion_example_with_linked_list.png 2x" data-file-width="2550" data-file-height="1808" /></a>/n<div class="thumbcaption">/n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mutual_exclusion_example_with_linked_list.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>/nA simple example of two processes modifying a linked list at the same time causing a conflict.
